B000E1ZBGS|5.0|Adaptations of this masterpiece have always seemed to me to idealize the period, as if everyone is always perfectly coiffed and nothing is ever out of place or even dusty. This film has filled in the story's background with great depth and color, taking us into a real English country dance, with all of its raucous energy. We see into people's homes with their daily activities, tasks, and yes, even the messes. The dialog feels like conversations that are face to face, not speeches posed across a giant stage. The overall result is a greater feeling of intimacy between you and the movie, almost as if it's not seen on screen but through a window... or even a mirror. I must have seen a dozen or more adaptations of this immortal story, but this time I felt like I was there, and a part of it all.<br /><br />As for the acting, I thought Matthew Macfadyen was the best Darcy I have ever seen. All of the actors were excellent, but I want to make special mention of Talulah Riley's performance as the bookish sister, Mary.  Most adaptations seem to disregard or even ridicule Mary for her social cluelessness. But this movie is brave enough to let us see her in greater depth and compassion, as a girl whose cluelessness is really just an extreme awkwardness, and therefore something we can all relate to. We even see her beauty and vulnerability as I have never seen on screen. In a master stroke of screenwriting, we see her suffer great pain and shame after her embarrassing attempt to be socially adept by performing at the pianoforte, albeit badly, to be publicly humiliated by her mother's loud declaration that \\"You have delighted us long enough\\" and her crumbling defeat from it. It is then that we see Mr. Bennet in a rare moment of warmth, comforting his disconsolate little girl, not aloof and sarcastic as usual, but holding her in his arms.  In this way, too, it breathed more life into the story in all of its fullness.<br /><br />That, for me, made this movie feel like the story was finally a part of myself, not just a tale about others.finally a part of myself, not just a tale about others.

But for Nightwish, not their best material imo.<br /><br />In this album, Tuomas and the crew have returned to their former glory 100%.  Anette now fits in perfectly with the style (imo in DPP, she was awesome but the music was in part begging for more operatics by design).  Some new tricks are wonderful surprises in Imaginaerum.  Anette's vocals are truly taken advantage of in this album, we get to hear more of a variety of styles which bring out her talent moreso than previously.  Marco, I almost didn't recognize his voice in one song, thought it was a guest singer at first, another wonderful surprise.  Yet in true Nightwish style they retain a consistent and overall alternating heavy and light theme, filled with a typical dark fantasy and deeply felt vibe, and very thoroughly layered and complex instruments.  Emppu has made me happy by adding a few more riffs than in DPP.  Jukka's drumming is very complexly enthralling and delightfully unpredictable, more like in the older albums I loved so much (some DPP songs, like Master Passion Greed, were repetitive and predictable, uncharacteristic for Nightwish). I'd have separated them with a few songs in between.  Also, I felt disappointed that there wasn't a true epic-length song in this album, as Nightwish usually does.  The Song of Myself was heading in that direction, but the second half of the 12 minute song is more or less actors reciting lines with light background music.  Don't get me wrong, Song of Myself is very fantastic, but only the first 6 minutes really count as a song.  Their lines were an interesting segment, but it's not really a song.  Imaginaerum the finale, should have been kept for the movie, as it's not really a song that works as a standalone metal band song, definitely something to put up to movie credits instead. B00005JKG9|5.0|Al Pacino, Jack Lemmon, Ed Harris, Alan Arkin, Kevin Spacey and Alec Baldwin head up this adaptation of an award-studded stage production. Naturally, given a cast so drenched with talent, the audience is going to want to see them flaunt their acting chops, and thankfully, that's the key to this entire production. Without a tight band of intense, versatile, believable characters at its core there's no chance a plot that revolves around sales calls and a bland office robbery would be anything more than a snoozer. But in this cast's hands, with this combination of sharp, passionate dialog and sleazy, self-serving lack of morality, it's totally entrancing. Like many play-to-film interpretations, there is a certain amount of stagnation in terms of the film's scenery. The vast majority of the action takes place in a sales office, with phone calls, police interviews and (de)motivational speakers providing the flavor. Those tame surroundings work in a variety of unusual ways, though: as a means to ground the story in reality, an exercise in diverting our attention solely to the acting, and a device to give the rare peeks outside the office's not-so-friendly confines an added dash of color. A master class in cadence and characterization, it's a deep, emphatic and intoxicating look at the gamut of techniques a desperate salesman can (and will) employ.
0307352145|4.0|The title of the book is very powerful though the book is more of an aspirational work. It includes a nice discussion about introverts and extraverts, how the world works for them, and of some of the contributing factors for the introverted personality types. The author quotes many relevant studies that casual readers and researchers will find interesting. Some of valuable findings for me were:<br />- introverts and extraverts have different stimulation levels (i.e. we all need some level of stimulation and the sweet spot is much higher for the extraverts than for the introverts)<br />- The U.S. generally encourages gregariousness and generally works best for extraverts (think classroom and office space setup, recruiting processes, focus on sales, popular culture, etc)<br />- Asia and Africa tend to be dominated by introverts whereas in part due to the U.S. and European histories of immigration, the latter (U.S. in particular) tend to be fertile ground for extraverts<br />- Adaptation & recognition are keys<br /><br />There aren't major discoveries in this book but it should be of some interest to introverts (describing the water to the fish) and of greater interest to employers, managers, parents, and anyone who is on the other side of the personality spectrum.

B0059XTUB8|4.0|Based on the biographical book authored by Colin Clark (Eddie Raymayne), the story follows the week he spent with Marilyn Monroe (Michelle Williams), while she was filming \\"The Prince and the Showgirl\\" in London. Upon Colin's graduation from university, he embarks on an opportunity to work on Lawrence Olivier's (Kennth Branaugh) new film, whom he had met at a party and is a mutual acquaintance. After much prodding with Hugh Perceval and Colin's persistence to arrive at Perceval's office everyday until a job is opened, Olivier comes in and offers him the opportunity of a lifetime. Colin's first task is to find a private residence for Marilyn while she is in London. He has found the perfect home secluded from prying eyes which impresses the primary film crew. Now with the trust and his passion to prove himself, his next project will be even a more bigger challenge. He is chosen to keep Marilyn company for a week while her husband, Arthur Miller, has to jet off back to America.<br />During the film we see Marilyn's insecurities and child like behavior. She does not enjoy being alone and with her flirtatiousness she seduces Colin. He is warned from those who know her best that her infatuation with him will not last.<br />Michelle Williams' portrayal of Marilyn was right on especially in a scene showing her dancing on the set as Olivier and the film crew watches in awe. Eddie Raymayne played the naive Clark very well showing how touching his relationship with Marilyn was. Watching the chemistry between the two of them I could see that Clark was very protective of Marilyn and did not want her to be hurt. He truly cared for her while at the same time he was enthralled with her stardom and beauty.

B007FXLZ9K|5.0|SHAME  is a brilliant, courageous, visually and emotionally stunning film by a new team of collaborators - director/writer Steve McQueen, cinematographer Sean Babbitt, and actor Michael Fassbender first gave us the unforgettable HUNGER and now they have teamed to present yet another unique work that will likely go down as one of the finest films of the decade.  Abi Morgan (The Hours television series, The Iron Lady, Tsunami, Sex Traffic) co wrote the screenplay with McQueen; they seem to have an affinity for each other.  This film has been controversial from the first mention that it was being made and controversy usually means that people are reacting strongly (both positively and negatively) to an art work - the true indication that this is by definition Art.<br /><br />The extraordinarily complex story of 21st century isolation and inability to connect with other people except on a unidirectional level of sexual contact is distilled in the role of Brandon (Michael Fassbender, in a multifaceted yet intensely focused performance): Brandon's life is dominated by near anonymous sexual encounters, internet pornography, and multiple daily escapes into onanism, He is quiet, handsome, and utterly irresistible to women and he takes advantage of this preoccupation with sexuality as a loner, living in an apartment, free of relationships, holding down a fine job as an account manager.  This changes when his sister Sissy (Carey Mulligan) `moves in' and her unwanted presence alters Brandon's modus operandi. Brandon's boss, the married with children Dave (James Badge Dale) envies Brandon's way with women, always losing in his unpracticed approaches to pickups to Brandon's superior techniques.  Dave meets Sissy at a nightclub where Sissy is singing (Mulligan's rendition of Kander and Ebb's `New York, New York' was apparently shot in one take at 3 AM while cameras focuses on the three actors - genuine responses to t a deeply moving moment) and ends up sleeping with Sissy. Brandon, frustrated with Sissy's presence and behavior ultimately leads to his throwing her out of his apartment - an act that has an impactful result. Meanwhile Brandon attempts to `relate' both emotionally and physically with fellow office worker Marianne (Nicole Beharie) with embarrassing results. And in his frustration he walks the night streets having sexual encounters with multiple partners out in the open, in a gay bar, and in thwarted attempts to pickup a girl whose boyfriend beats him severely.<br /><br />Much of the success of this art piece is due to the manner in which McQueen presents his story.  Many of the scenes ore simply faces on the subway and Brandon's fantasies, all accompanied by a musical score by Harry Escott who has elected to use multiple recordings of Glenn Gould playing Bach's Goldberg Variations and Preludes and Fugues - the sublime simplicity of Bach's statements in marked contrast to the chaos in Brandon's mind and actions. In a splendid scene as Brandon awaits a subway, when his mind is tortured and out of control cinematographer Babbitt distorts the image like an acid trip.  But most impressive is the choice to concentrate on Brandon's face as he eyes tricks or faces the world in which he lives in his out of control state  - a face so misguided and needy and yet sensuous that it makes the film draw us inside a character's psyche like few other films have been able.<br /><br />Michael Fassbender does the impossible; he creates a role that despite the behavioral abnormalities remains a man about whom we care about deeply. At film's beginning there is subtle male frontal nudity and Fassbender's remarks about that are mature and enlightening: `To be honest with you again, I think it's the idea of male frontal nudity. It just baffles me: Women can parade around naked all the time, but the guy conveniently has his pants on. I remember my mom always complaining about that to me, saying, \\"This is such `b-s', it's always the women who are naked\\" ...so I did this one for you, Mom!  Everyone involved in this film is to be commended for the highest level of artistic achievement.  And that extends to the credits at film's end: each participant's name is flashed on the screen with a single musical chord on the piano. The effect is respectful and in keeping with the brilliance of the movie.  Grady Harp, April 10so I did this one for you, Mom!  Everyone involved in this film is to be commended for the highest level of artistic achievement.  And that extends to the credits at film's end: each participant's name is flashed on the screen with a single musical chord on the piano. The effect is respectful and in keeping with the brilliance of the movie.  Grady Harp, April 10

B005C7SXMS|3.0|\\"Submarine\\" has, at least to my reading, been compared to 1998's classic, \\"Rushmore.\\"  Both movies reek of the indie spirit and feature struggling prep-school quasi-outcasts whose delusions of self-worth fall dizzyingly short of reality.  But where \\"Rushmore\\" was saved by the innate goodness of its hero and the movie's charmingly whimsical tone, \\"Submarine\\" fails to rise to the heights of the Wes Anderson film.<br /><br />Oliver Tate is our hero in \\"Submarine,\\" a struggling teenager in the mid-1980s. A victim of a cruel hoax by the love of his young life, Oliver surprisingly finds himself in the catbird seat.  What ensues as Oliver confronts increasingly melodramatic plto points is an exercise in Oliver's complete lack of social skills and genuine empathy for his fellow man.  It's not that Oliver doesn't consider other people's feelings - it's that he thinks about them and almost invariably draws the wrong conclusion.  While many have found the movie to be both poignant and on the mark, too many key points seemed too contrived to sustain my interest.  I can suspend disbelief and will even believe the ridiculous in the context of a good movie, but \\"Submarine\\" is too clever and shocking by half.<br /><br />There are far worse coming of age tales out there, but there are many that are superior.

1451648537|4.0|Steve Jobs was a boor, a bully and a manipulative control freak. He was also a visionary who stood at the intersection of technology, culture and consumer marketing and almost single-handedly pulled the rest of us into the future he imagined. Jobs and the brilliant teams he assembled reinvented the consumer experience in personal computing, animated movies, music, telephony and portable digital devices.  In the process he created what is currently the world's most valuable company.<br /><br />Most of the information in this book is common knowledge to anyone who bought a computer or read a magazine over the past three decades.  It's all here, from the creation myth of Apple's birth in a Silicon Valley garage, the launch of the Mac, Jobs being bounced by Apple's board in favor of John Sculley, the very productive years in the wilderness running Pixar and Next, culminating in the triumphant return to Apple and the dazzling run of iproduct successes.<br /><br />The book makes clear why Jobs flourished in business.  He knew exactly what he wanted to accomplish, and knew how to attract top notch people to execute his ideas.  Besides seeing the big picture, but could dive down to the tiniest detail, a rare combination.  He wasn't afraid to bet the business to make a great product or reverse direction to improve a lesser one.  Despite the abuse he heaped on his closest associates, they stayed loyal to his vision of making integrated products that tightly controlled the user experience.  Jobs often said that his greatest creation was the development culture he birthed at Apple.<br /><br />Genius is harder to explain than business success.  Jobs had an intuitive, almost mystical ability to visualize the direction of consumer technology, along with the drive and charisma to turn his insights into cool products.  He was tuned into product design from an early age, and a fanatic quest for the perfect form in all its manifestations drove him his entire life.  Although young Jobs had a hippie ethos, being into Zen, vegetarianism, rock music, among other things, he also displayed a sort of emotional Tourette's: he would say in an unfiltered way exactly what he thought about anything the instant he thought it.  This saddened his close friends.  It should have sunk his career, and almost did, but he combined it with an instinctive sense of other's needs, along with a ruthless ability to use what he saw to get what he wanted. He was a Zen master of intermittent reinforcement, the most sophisticated form of human manipulation and the most difficult to resist. Given his drive, his manipulative abilities and his genius at mass communication (he created several of the most brilliant ad campaigns of the last fifty years) we are perhaps fortunate he didn't decide to go into politics.<br /><br />The people who worked with Jobs or competed against him, such as Bill Gates, describe his actions but are less good at making us feel what they felt in his presence.  I wish Isaacson had spent more time with the women in his life, especially his first real love, Chrisann Brennan, with whom he had a daughter, Lisa, with Lisa herself, with his significant girlfriends and with his wife Luanne, who is well regarded by Isaacson, but not pushed to reveal much about living for two decades with a man widely regarded as impossible on his best days.  After being diagnosed with cancer Jobs became a little more vulnerable and reflective, but still continued to treat the world as an obstacle to his vision of what it might become if only people would fall in line with his will.<br /><br />Given his business and product successes, Isascson's assessment that Steve Jobs was the Thomas Edison or Henry Ford of his time seems valid.  Culturally, his legacy is less certain.  Walk down the streets in any city and see all the people plugged in to their Apple devices, minimally engaged with their environment.  Apple promotes its products as empowering, but they are empowering in thesolipsistic way that fantasy games are:  you are captain of your digital ship, but navigating in your head is not the same as navigating on the seas of real life.  The kind of closed systems architecture Jobs insisted on developing may not be the best model for a sprawling, multi-cultural democracy where people can only go forward by forging messy compromises.  The asocial self-absorption of jacking into to your personal apps and playlists while the world swirls around you may be the darker but truer legacy of Jobs' monomaniacal genius.solipsistic way that fantasy games are:  you are captain of your digital ship, but navigating in your head is not the same as navigating on the seas of real life.  The kind of closed systems architecture Jobs insisted on developing may not be the best model for a sprawling, multi-cultural democracy where people can only go forward by forging messy compromises.  The asocial self-absorption of jacking into to your personal apps and playlists while the world swirls around you may be the darker but truer legacy of Jobs' monomaniacal genius.

B0000024SI|5.0|Well, this is certainly the best album to come out of 1965 (though it's probably actually tied with Rubber Soul- if I remember there's a difference between the American and British versions of Rubber Soul and I prefer the American one despite the shorter length... but this isn't about the Beatles!)<br /><br />It should be noted that, despite how much I love this album, I have no problem admitting that the amount of creative lyrics presented throughout Highway 61 Revisited are probably WAY out of my league.  I can't really explain the meaning behind any of these songs, though the truth is, the way I listen to music is that I interpret lyrics in a way that relates to how *I* think about them and disregard the meaning.  I feel this same with *all* my favorite artists.  Oh well.  With that rubbish out of the way, let's get started!<br /><br />\\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" is the song that radio stations have been beating into our eardrums for several years now.  Actually I can't remember the last time I heard it on the radio.  Every time I turn on the radio I hear Katy Perry, Pink and that sort of exciting and danceable pop *snickers*.  Anyway, I think I speak for several people when I say every single lyric of \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" is basically memorized in my head.  I bet you're sitting out there reading this and can claim to having memorized every word of this amazing song (whether intentionally or not since you've probably heard it several hundreds if not thousands of times).  Oh, and remember what I say about about interpreting lyrics in MY own way?  Either it's because of stubbornness or shallowness, but I definitely like to think of the line \\"How does it feel to be on your own, with no direction home\\" as a moment of freedom and the excitement and confusion that soon follows.<br /><br />\\"Tombstone Blues\\" mentions all sorts of lyrics about Paul Revere, Jack the Ripper, the commander in chief, geometry and who knows what else.  What's especially interesting is how it actually has a chorus with the same phrase repeated several times, something Dylan normally avoided implementing.  I've always believed he avoided lyrical repetition because he's too darn good at being a walking talking dictionary so such a tactic is beneath him, heh.  Anyway, you'll definitely hear the line \\"mama's in the factory she ain't got no shoes, daddy's in the alley he's lookin for food, I'm in the kitchen with the tombstone blues!\\" more than once, that's for sure.  My favorite line is \\"the sun's not yellow- it's chicken!\\"  Whatever encouraged him to come up with THAT hilarious line is beyond me!  Then again, maybe it's not supposed to be funny.  Or then again, I'm inadvertently taking it out of context anyway thanks to the complexity of the lyrical matter Dylan unleashed with this album.<br /><br />\\"It Takes a Lot to Laugh, It Takes a Train to Cry\\" is a slow-moving blues tune with tasty piano arrangements.  Normally this kind of song would be ordinary but Dylan has that magical ability (perhaps due to the sound of his voice alone) to make it rather interesting and worth your time.  Plus his voice sort of sails in a unique way after the verse.  \\"From a Buick 6\\" is a rocker about a woman who apparently will do anything for her man!  Gotta love those types of women!  \\"Ballad of a Thin Man\\" is absolutely incredible for one thing- the atmosphere.  An *enormous* atmosphere that's so powerful and haunting... and imaginative.  Doesn't it make you think about prisoners walking slowly down death row, or a detective in a smoky room working on a case alone?  A song that stands out drastically I believe.  The best song on the album too.<br /><br />\\"Queen Jane Approximately\\" is slightly similar to \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" in sound but of course, lyrically it's from an entirely different planet.  These lyrics are actually relatable to me and they seem to describe MY family!  Hey that can't be a coincidence!  Well it has to be.  How did Dylan know 45 years ago that my family would turn out the way he describes in this song?  The title song is a fast-paced rocker with memorable vocals.  This song really makes me wanna drive down the highway and just blast it at the highest possible volume level that my speakers will allow, haha.  \\"Just Like Tom Thumb's Blues\\" is a slower-paced, piano based tune that's probably more melodic than most of the material here.  I love the final verse melody about going back to New York City.  \\"Desolation Row\\" is hard to interpret.  I guess it's about loneliness and Dylan covers a variety of different situations and people to accurately get this point across (such as Einstein, Robin Hood, mermaids, commissioners, maids and letters).  Let's not overlook the creative acoustic guitar arrangements in the background either- that guitar is just beautiful here.  Another highlight.<br /><br />Overall, Dylan's lyrical masterpiece.  Even surpassing Blonde on Blonde in the lyrics department.

B000001DQG|5.0|You know the one thing that's quite unusual about Caribou is how the bonus tracks are organized.  I mean right after the depressingly downbeat \\"Don't Let the Sun Go Down on Me\\" and the tragic and sad story of a man who walks into a bar and shoots several people in \\"Ticking\\", we're treated to a more upbeat and positive atmosphere with Elton's cover of \\"Pinball Wizard\\".  Right after the grim nature of \\"Cold Highway\\" that mentions a story concerning a dangerous road where one of Bernie's friends was killed, we're treated to the upbeat and happy \\"Step Into Christmas\\".  After this song -if you happen to have your CD on repeat- it goes back to the beginning and \\"The Bitch Is Back!\\"  The order of the tracks is just really strange, haha.<br /><br />Well to be honest with you, perhaps since I love everything Elton John has done in the 70's anyway (with the exception of the disastrous disco sellout that is Victim of Love which I pretend doesn't exist) I truthfully can't find any really major faults with Caribou.  While it's true the average music fan would find tracks such as \\"Grimsby\\" and \\"Dixie Lily\\" as pure filler, the gigantic Elton John fan in me believes otherwise!  These are just innocent, colorfully arranged simplistic country songs that are actually totally appropriate for the month I'm writing this review- mid-April when the flowers are growing and spring is in full blossom.  In the case of \\"Grimsby\\", you can definitely tell it's a rushed effort thanks to the lazy way Elton sings the chorus.  When you buy the remastered version of Caribou, you will read that the band only had a little more than a week to make the entire album.  With THAT in mind, isn't it quite fascinating that the music is as good as it is?<br /><br />I used to HATE \\"The Bitch Is Back\\".  It took many years for me to warm up to the horns and the funky style that seemed unnatural to me for the longest time.  I love it now.  Funny how our tastes can change so dramatically as we grow up.  \\"Pinky\\" is awonderful ballad with... oh alright I'll put aside my Elton fanboy ways and admit that the arrangements are fairly standard.  I still love it though.  The chorus is especially beautiful with the pleasant \\"ahhhhh\\" humming in the background that's an Elton trademark.  I love that sound.  I never could figure out if that's Elton singing that way or another band member.  Nobody can understand a single word from \\"Solar Prestige a Gammon\\" and guess what?  According to the notes you're not supposed to, haha.  I thought it was German!  I don't know WHAT it is- all I know is that the chorus is wonderful and reminds me of sitting outside an Italian pizza place.  \\"You're So Static\\" contains a REALLY good chorus- it repeats quite a bit, but I love it anyway.  The horns give it character.<br /><br />\\"I've Seen the Saucers\\" is another example of quality writing that should have been more well-known when you listen carefully to the vocals- they are definitely hit-worthy.  \\"Stinker\\" used to BE really stinky to me but now that I've warmed up to the funky horns that occupy a good portion of the album, I don't find a track like this nearly as annoying as I once did.  It's probably a couple minutes overlong, though.  We all know the hit \\"Don't Let the Sun Go Down on Me\\" and I'm probably the only person on the planet that believes the verse melody and the dramatic and decorative piano arrangements are the moments that *make* this song so darn good.  Most people will say this is Elton's way of warming up before getting to the chorus, but the way Elton stretches his range really is a fascinating thing.  Who knows, perhaps being left-handed makes me think the wrong way, lol.<br /><br />\\"Ticking\\" as I mention above, is a compelling song with lyrics that seem to predict the troubled minds of the young generation and the way they create unexplainable violence with guns and revenge.  Back in 1974 lyrics like these were probably considered an unlikely tragedy but nowadays?  This stuff happens all the time, I'm afraid.  Just turn on the news and see.  I love how Elton REALLY gets into it- as the song rolls along even the piano playing takes an aggressive and angry turn as Elton builds his emotions and expresses the surreal lyrics as chillingly as possible.  A VERY haunting song.<br /><br />The bonus tracks... well I definitely like \\"Step Into Christmas\\" the most.  It seems lately that it's cool to bash a song like this.  I'm not sure why either.  I mean, when Christmas comes around I always though \\"Step Into Christmas\\" was right up there with a certain John Lennon and Emerson, Lake & Palmer song in terms of \\"Best Christmas songs by rock artists\\" but I guess most people think otherwise these days.  I love it though.  \\"Step into Christmas, the admission is free-e-e!\\"  I never really thought too highly of Elton's version of \\"Pinball Wizard\\".  He seems to take his vocal range to a REALLY unnatural and helium-voiced level and it's definitely not as good as the Who's version, that's for sure.  \\"Cold Highway\\" has very good vocals and \\"Sick City\\" has a memorable chorus for the way the jazzy arrangements give it some juice.<br /><br />Overall, I love Caribou, and it's honestly only one small step down in quality compared to Goodbye Yellow Brick Road.

0786955570|5.0|Like many here, I played D&D when I was younger but have drifted away and lost touch with most of the people I used to play with. My wife recently mentioned that she would like to give D&D a try, and I figured that this game would be an excellent way to dip our toes in without the need for a dedicated group of players, a huge time commitment and hundreds of pages of rulebooks. I ended up getting it as a birthday present a few days ago, and so far it has lived up to expectations.<br /><br />The best thing for us is that everyone plays a hero; there is no need for a DM. In fact, the game can even be played solo, though that takes most of the fun out of it. The dungeon is generated randomly using interlocking tiles, and monsters and traps are controlled by the players using simple scripts. There are also no character sheets; everything is kept track of using cards and little cardboard counters. This is a dungeon crawl plain and simple with very little actual role-playing involved. You can go up to level 2 within an adventure, but there is no continuity between adventures. You simply pick one of the pre-defined characters and keep slaying monsters until you meet whatever the particular adventure's objective is, then pick a new adventure in no particular order and start again next time. While this limits the complexity of the game quite a bit, it also makes it totally \\"pick up and play\\" and eliminates the logistical nightmares of getting together a dedicated group of players on a regular basis. You can simply have a \\"game night\\" and whoever shows up grabs a character and jumps in.<br /><br />From our experience with our first couple of adventures, this game can be quite challenging. Your characters are subjected to a huge amount of punishment, including constant attacks from monsters and \\"encounters\\" that are almost always something nasty. Hit points become a precious resource to be jealously husbanded, and healing surges (which are like continues in a video game) go quickly. If even one player dies with no healing surges left, it's game over. However, you can adjust the difficulty by changing the number of healing surges that you start out with. For our first couple of games we decided to go with 3 instead of the recommended 2, which turned out to be a good idea.<br /><br />The game box itself is massive and contains a ton of components, including 41 heavy card stock dungeon tiles, 42 plastic miniatures (including a massive skeletal dragon), and 200 Encounter, Monster and Treasure cards. Everything seems to be high quality, and though the miniatures are mass-produced plastic and probably not the highest quality miniatures available, they get the job done nicely. I'm sure if you were so inclined you would be able to use these figures and tiles for a regular D&D campaign. There is also a ton of room for additions to the standard rules and adventures included in this box. In addition to the 13 adventures included in the box, there are a number available for free download from the Wizards of the Coast website. You can also make up your own fairly easily, and the basic rules are simple enough that you can add your own house rules to suit your tastes.<br /><br />Overall, I'm very happy with this product. If you are a hard core roleplayer you will probably complain that this is too simple, but for people like me the fact that it is simple and self-contained is a big part of its appeal. I can happily report that my wife, who has never played this type of game before, has enjoyed herself every time we've played and is excited about trying out more adventures. We will be getting a ton of use out of this.

0061914177|2.0|The advice in this book can be summarized as follows:<br /><br />1) Quit your boring job.<br />2) Create a blog about your passion, and dedicate a smallish percentage of your time to creating very high quality, engaging content.<br />3) Dedicate a largish amount of time to promoting your personal \\"brand\\" by commenting on other blogs, forums, and newsgroups related to your passion as well as publicizing your blog through social media.<br />4) Your new status as an online guru will result in money making opportunities.<br /><br />The challenges of this plan are unfortunately myriad.  As Gary concedes fairly late in the book, most people (by definition) aren't able to create the engaging content that's going to establish themselves as a leader in the field, especially if you're only dedicating a small percentage of your time to its production. You'll be lucky to make $30K a year working 80hr weeks following the plan Gary says, but hey, better to making $30k doing something you love than $60k doing something you hate.  I guess it's up to the reader to decide.<br /><br />The most glaring omission is almost any discussion of numbers at all. There are plenty glorified shout outs like \\"Suzy created a blog about sewing and now she's crushing it!\\". How many monthly visitors does Suzy have? How much money is Suzy making? How is she making it? All these questions go unanswered.  Gary's own blog piggybacked off an existing and rapidly growing wine business started by his father, but it's very hard to tell from the book how much his blogging activity has helped (or hindered for that matter) its success. The world of online blogging is fiercely competitive and saturated with content. Standing out isn't easy. It would be interesting to know what percentage of full time bloggers are able to make a living off their labor.<br /><br />The most eye opening assertion came late in the book. Gary mentions someone who had worked on his blog for two years without establishing much of a following. His response? These things take time! At the very least, that makes Gary's plan an extremely time consuming and expensive experiment. I really wish he included advice on how to create a \\"weekend blog\\" that would shed some light on whether you have the right stuff.  Maybe he needs to team up with the folks over at \\"The Lean Startup\\".<br /><br />If you want to get fired up about using social media to create a personal brand, this is probably worth a read. If you're looking to \\"cash in on your passion\\", I'd consider a book like \\"The Lean Startup\\" or even \\"The 4-Hour Workweek\\" which outline a more nuts and bolts approach to online earning. \\"Crush It!\\" asks us to take too much on faith.

B004Y5AV4M|5.0|Venetia is the story of a beautiful but lonely heroine who meets her Mr. Rochester and, despite all odds, wins him in the end.  This is the classic Georgette Heyer novel in that it has all the right ingrediants.  There is a beautiful and intelligent heroine counterbalanced by the slightly dissipated hero who has lost his faith in the world and in love.  Add to this mixture the right number of flawed characters from the mainly just totally self-centered to the down-right nasty and you have a well-rounded tale of love and romance.  But the main spice of this particular Georgette Heyer novel is the repartee between Venetia and her love, Lord Dameral.  From the first stolen kiss to the passionate denouement, this is the electricity that sets this novel apart from her others.  Ths final scene when the lovers are reunited is reminisent of Jane reconciling with Mr. Rochester and demonstrates more passion than is usually seen in her stories.<br /><br />Altogether, this is one of Ms. Heyer's finer novels and is a thoroughly good read from start to end.

In preparation for an upcoming trip, I got both the Scotland book and the London book. Both are fantastic.<br /><br />This book features the top 16 things to see in London, complete with descriptions and photographs of those places. There is a calender that tells you important local events, to help you plan your trip. It lists restaurants and museums, theaters and operas, hotels and places to stay, and all manner of shops. It tells you the best ways to get around. It breaks down the city by sector, such as West End, South Bank, Nottinghill, Greenwich, and so forth. There's a section that helps you plan day trips away from London, if you want to see nearby sights, such as Oxford, Brighton, Windsor, or Bath. It even goes into the architecture, history, art, fashion, music, etc., of the city, so you can learn everything there is to know about London. Best of all, there is a full-sized pull-out map at the back of the book, which is a great help.<br /><br />I'm quite pleased with this book. I've just skimmed through it, but I've learned a great deal. It will make planning our brief trip to London much easier, helping us to decide what we must see and what can wait for the next trip. Without it, we would be utterly lost and wouldn't have a clue where to go, what to see, or how to get there. Five stars and a wholehearted recommendation!

B0079TQHDO|5.0|This is the second book in a page-turning family saga chronicle of 5 books by master story teller, Jeffrey Archer. The story covers the lives of the Clifton and Barrington families who live in and around Bristol in England and is this book is set during the time of the Second World War, both in England and America.<br /><br />The secret of Jeffrey Archer's success, despite his flawed personal history, is that he has always been is one of the best story tellers around. He tells a tale and then weaves another tale around it. The characters, both good and bad, are skilfully crafted and become essential elements in telling his tales. Before you know it you are totally hooked on what is going to happen next.<br /><br />In this book we follow the life of Harry Clifton, the son of a poor docker who transformed himself from an under-achieving child of a poor family to be able to enter Oxford University.  At the start of the Second World War, to get sufficient experience to be accepted for the Navy, Harry leaves Oxford to work as a junior officer on a cargo ship. When the ship is torpedoed by a U Boat in the North Atlantic he is rescued by an American passenger ship. In an attempt to release himself from insoluble personal and emotional problems with the Barrington family back in England, Harry takes the identity of another officer he hardly knows who drowned in the attack. When he arrives in New York under the assumed name he is immediately arrested for murder.<br /><br />Once again Harry's courageous character and intelligence allow him to cope with life in jail and overcome its limitations. While in jail he keeps a detailed diary that will become the secret of his later success.<br /><br />Back in England, his family, and most of the Barringtons are devastated to hear that Harry Clifton has drowned. Harry's mother receives a letter from someone connected with the rescue but because she is illiterate she refuses to open it. His girl friend Emma cannot believe the news and travels to New York to find out what really happened.<br /><br />Meanwhile back in England Emma's unscrupulous father Hugo lives a life of deceit,  insolvency and lies. When his father dies, leaving him to head the family shipping line, Hugo's mismanagement rapidly brings the family fortunes to the edge of insolvency. Meanwhile his son Giles leaves Oxford to join the Army and becomes a hero of the seige of Tobruk.<br /><br />To tell more would spoil the reader's enjoyment of the tale. While I really enjoyed the first book, Only Time Will Tell, I thought this book was better and I couldn't put it down. Once again Archer leaves you with an unfinished dramatic development making one anxious to read the next book straight away. Other reviewers see this as a fault - I find it a challenging way of moving to the next book in the saga. My greatest concern is that it may be over a year before the next book is published, and then Archer will leave me wanting to read the next one....!<br /><br />This is not really a stand alone book as many of the developments depend on a knowledge of the previous book. I highly recommend that if possible your should read Only Time Will Tell before reading this book.travels to New York to find out what really happened.<br /><br />Meanwhile back in England Emma's unscrupulous father Hugo lives a life of deceit,  insolvency and lies. B006ZT47O0|5.0|For people new to Epica, I can only describe their music as a mix between symphony, choir, metal, and a front woman with one of the most amazing voices I've ever heard. Out of all Epica's albums, I easily rate this the best, with a toss up between, \\"The Divine Conspiracy\\" and \\"Design Your Universe\\" for second.<br /><br />For a bit of background about my musical tastes, I'll just say this: Epica is one of only 3 bands I listen to daily like a routine... no more like a drug. The other two are Scale the Summit and Animals as Leaders. I'm into heavier music, but not too heavy, that I can get lost in and just think while my brain follows the patterns of the drums. Instrumentally, this album gave me everything I look for in music, and more, with songs like: Monopoly on Truth, Storm the Sorrow, Requiem for the Indifferent, Deter the Tyrant, and Serenade of Self destruction. With care for the reader's time, I'll spend the remainder of the review with a quick blurb about what the song is and why I like it, without focusing too much on what I think are weaknesses of the album, as I think this would be a waste of time. Art is subjective, and you may listen to the album and think, \\"What was he talking about?\\" All I can really speak on is how and why these songs touched me, with hopes that this will give you something to look forward to when listening to the album!<br /><br />Monopoly on Truth: Starts out with an interesting vocal style, pounding drums, but otherwise kind of a basic metal song... that is until about 3:30 into the song. Then the entire song seems to change. The chorus changes and is sung by a choir, the lyrics are interesting and the feel of the song is just... EPIC. Very memorable and a song I always look forward to on my playlist, not matter my mood.<br /><br />Storm the Sorrow: A slower song, supposed to be the \\"pop\\" song that Epica has a tradition of putting on their albums. See Unleashed for a similar example. What got me about the song is the drastic changein vocals, and the lyrics! I usually don't \\"get\\" poetry, and am terrible at it, but I almost felt on some level that this song was talking about me, which I've never felt about a song before.<br /><br />Requiem for the Indifferent: This song is kind of like the \\"Kingdom of Heaven\\" for this song, complete with everything down to the in your face chorus and prog-like time changes. The intro to this song is absolutely amazing! (think the acoustic riff from Martyr of the Free Word, combined with the Middle Eastern sounding instruments from Fools of Damnation). The rest of the song doesn't disappoint, if you can get past the politically charged lyrics. It isn't a song I listen to every time I put in the album, but I enjoy it when I do.<br /><br />Deter the Tyrant: When I first heard this song, my very fist though was WOW THAT KEYBOARD! OMG THOSE DRUUUUUUMS! Then when I picked the rational side of my brain up off the floor, all I could think was, \\"Why aren't there more songs like this?\\" Seriously, epic instruments with a very metal feel, a woman singer with a gorgeous voice (A welcome detour from all the screaming, growling, super high pitched guy singers as of late). In short, this is almost everything I ever want from a song unless I'm in the mood for Animals as Leaders, which is a WHOLE different type of music for me entirely.<br /><br />Serenade of Self Destruction: Wow that intro! Seriously, it's getting more intense? Wooo go double bass go! Drive that symphony! And then the intro ends. I forgot to mention the hauntingly beautiful piano at the very beginning. I didn't like how the awesome instrumental stopped in favor of a choir singing lyrics I had a hard time understanding, but when the instruments were there, they were great!

Having been a wanderer of many a bookstore and library in my youth, the days of casually browsing through shelves of familiar names are all-but over and a beloved past-time has been desaturated with tired angles and rehashed stories.<br /><br />Breaking the mold is one Rebecca Hamilton, a rare gem picked up by Immortal Ink Publishing for her first book, The Forever Girl.<br /><br />I'll be honest, I happened across an interview with her on a blog that was offering a free copy of the book, so I thought I'd give it a shot. With a complete stroke of luck, I ended up winning the copy and did a ridiculous happy dance that lasted all of 3.2 seconds before I realized nobody was there to witness my gloating. (Self-gratification is for suckers)<br /><br />So on to the book itself -<br /><br />The Forever Girl follows the life of twenty-two year old Sophia Parsons, a practicing Wiccan in a small, religious town. Not her religion. Already being set apart as an outsider, things complicate when she can't seem to escape a constant buzzing in her head.<br /><br />From there, Sophia's attempts at deeper understanding lead into a well-crafted world of supernatural beings. Not your typical vampire/werewolf nonsense that has taken paranormal romance by storm and run rampant and streaking through the quad with all the appeal of a sun-burned manatee...<br /><br />Ahem...<br /><br />Instead, Rebecca has woven together an amazing mythology that holds through out the story. Her ability to write tension is some of the best I've ever seen, painting panic with words to accompany the rich, vibrant hues of the world she's crafted.<br /><br />My advice? Pick up this book and call in sick, because it's damned near impossible to find a stopping point.

0385534639|4.0|First just one quick note about reviews: just because you didn't like it, doesn't mean it's automatically a bad book/poorly written/etc. that isn't worth the hype. No book is everyone's cup of tea. Reviews should be used to judge if it's a book that YOU would enjoy. Like many other things, a book can still contain flaws and yet be enjoyed immensely if it is a good fit for you.<br /><br />So the question you should ask is not whether Night Circus is \\"good\\" or \\"bad,\\" but whether it is something you would enjoy. So here's my impressions to help you with your judgement. Overall the book captivated me, but I can still see why some of the other reviewers weren't as fond of it:<br /><br />The start of Night Circus begins in second-person(i.e. \\"you\\"), but soon switches back and forth between many other different points of views. Some readers have found this to be jarring, but it wasn't something that bothered me. If you approach the story looking for one central narrative, then all the jumping around might irk you, but I saw it as many related storylines slowly unveiling and revealing aspects about the other storylines. There were a couple of points of views and parts of the story that at first seemed to come from left-field (like a character named Bailey) and appeared to be random and irrelevant, but if you read to the end, it all comes together.<br /><br />The beginning of the book, after that second-person POV chapter, will likely draw you in immediately with an intriguing set up. The climax and build up to it towards the end of the book is gripping. It's the middle part that I can see why some people might give up on the book. With all the switching back and forth in POV and chapters where nothing 'important' seems to happen, it's going to come as a snooze for some folks and the characters, though striking, come across as not being very well developed. While in retrospect I can recognize how people came to perceive said flaws, none of it was even vaguely registered as Icontinued to flip the pages.<br /><br />You see Night Circus is a book where the setting takes center stage. This is probably a circus you've never seen/heard/read about. The imagery of the circus and the magic is incredibly enthralling, vivid, unique, and at many points the setting is the focus in a scene. If you fall under its spell, you keep reading to experience the world of the Night Circus a little longer and to unravel the mysteries of the people who are involved with it. It's been a while since an imaginative setting filled me with such a sense of wonder. There are novels that are character driven and as such need incredibly well-developed characters in order to succeed, but there are also engrossing novels that are setting-driven where the focus isn't on a character's growth. So while Night Circus's cast might be perceived as \\"flat characters\\" in that they don't get too much in-depth development, they are certainly not dull characters, but a colorful component that adds to the intrigue of the magical circus and the mystery that unfolds that is part dream and part nightmare.<br /><br />But for some of you, the magic will not be enough and instead you will find it excessive and probably self-indulgent. For others like me, it was a breath-taking journey and a world I wished I could experience for real. If there's even a bit of a dreamer or a lover of fantastical settings in you, definitely give this book a try.

B002LLE3PY|3.0|I'll be direct: \\"Lizard\\" is my least-favorite King Crimson album. I don't like Gordon Haskell's singing. I don't especially like his bass playing, either, or Andy McCulloch's drumming. I don't like the fact that all the album was written by guitarist Robert Fripp and lyricist Pete Sinfield; there's a sameness here that's a far cry from the experimentation of \\"In the Court of the Crimson King\\" or even \\"In the Wake of Poseidon.\\"<br /><br />\\"Lizard\\" was KC's third studio album, and came after a major personnel shakeup that saw the departure of bassist/vocalist Greg Lake, saxophonist Ian McDonald and drummer Michael Giles. This album has been called an acquired taste, and I agree. But without this album, King Crimson might have broken up for good, precluding the later releases that were so good, such as \\"Red,\\" \\"Discipline\\" and \\"Thrak.\\"<br /><br />It IS nice to hear this difficult, transitional album in the 5.1 mix created by Porcupine Tree's Steven Wilson; sonically, at least, the album doesn't sound as muddy and confused as the original. For the most part, though: This album is for King Crimson completists -- dedicated fans who have worked their way through the rest of the catalog and are curious about how the band survived its first personnel crisis.

B003XTUZIM|4.0|...this 40th anniversary remix of \\"Islands\\" does present the material in its best possible light. One of the standout tracks, \\"Ladies of the Road,\\" is far, far raunchier than most of the King Crimson catalog, but at the time this record was released, the band desperately needed to show it still had some fire in its belly -- KC already had come close to disbanding more than once by the time \\"Islands\\" was recorded.<br /><br />Part of the problem with \\"Islands\\" and its predecessor, \\"Lizard,\\" is that there's simply no overarching vision for the album; the musical styles wander all over the place, from rock to progressive to jazzy. For other groups, that's not a problem, but King Crimson always needed to sound unified, whether they were exploring the airy prog-rock textures of \\"In the Court of the Crimson King\\" or loud/louder dynamic of \\"Thrak.\\"<br /><br />This release will be of most interest to listeners who can play back the DVD-Audio disc, on which Steven Wilson has remixed the sound for 5.1, so that each instrument can be heard more clearly. There's also a CD with just the stereo mix, but frankly, if I were not a DVD-A guy, I would've stuck with my 30th Anniversary version. Bottom line: There's reason to buy this if you're a multichannel listener and a Crimson completist.

B001GSTOAM|5.0|Before reading this book I used to develop ad-hoc, repeated code several times across several modules. The architecture where I currently work doesn't help in the reusability matter, there isn't a common module. But anyway, this book has shown me the real cost of dirty code and how I wasn't helping at all my sucessors and team mates and above all it has shown me how I was acting unprofessionally.<br />The book, inside its medium constraints (display, formatting, page size, etc.) is very thorough into showing examples of bad coding and how to refactor them. It talks about some principles and practices which help into creating clean code, pherhaps others books elaborate those principles (Single Responsability for instance) in greater detail and the author gives references to those books. Of course each project has its own characteristics, it is up to developer to apply the practices according to the circumstances. You will have to think a lot to find out which patterns would achieve a cleaner code. But, at least for me, it has helped me building components that are easier to mantain and change. And when I write clean code it feels like the computer is working for me, what would first require several Ctrl+C and Ctrl+V to implement and much more keystrokes to change, now require just some refactoring (in Eclipse IDE) but also a lot of brainpower, which much better used at refactoring rather than in understanding (namely reading lots of classes, endless scrolling, remembering where is the function that you need,etc. just to understand a single method)<br />So, in short, it is a terrific starting point to produce clean code (easier to understand, change and mantain), that will not hinder yourself or your teamates later, but it is in no way a one-size-fits-all formula, In fact there isn't such thing, at least in my opinion.

B0073XORCY|4.0|In 1972 Gerald \\"Little Milton\\" Bostock, an 8 year old English school boy,  wrote a poem and won a poetry contest with that poem. The poem was \\"Thick As A Brick\\", and chronicled the hardships of growing up. But then something terrible happened. Gerald and his poem were disqualified after a multitude of protests and threats concerning the offensive nature of the poem, furthered by allegations of the boy's psychological instability. It seems young Gerald used the word \\"g__r\\" during a television broadcast, and to add flame to the fire, a 14-year-old girl named Julia Fealey became pregnant and claimed Gerald was expert at more than poetry. This whole tawdry episode was chronicled in The St. Cleve Chronicle and Linwell Advertiser, a local village Newspaper filled with the usual parochial and amateurish  journalism. The story was on the front page along with an article about an `experimental non-rabbit', a rather naughty connect-the-dots children's activity, the complete, non abridged poem itself ,as well as a review of an album by, the soon to be labeled \\"progressive rock\\" but for now blues/jazz/rock band, Jethro Tull who cut an album with \\"Little Milton's\\" complete poem as its one and only lyric.<br /><br />Now, all of the above is legend, if you haven't figured that out yet...except for the album being cut by the `progressive' rock band, Jethro Tull. By 1972, Jethro Tull were gaining international recognition as sort of a second wave in the British Invasion. The story of Gerald Bostock, and the ensuing row over his poem, not to mention his success with Ms. Fealey were all the product of Ian Anderson's mind. After all, just how much could an 8 year old poetry prodigy tell us about growing up? And the album, Thick As a Brick was, itself, a satirical spoof. Ian Anderson was surprised by the critical reaction to the previous album, Aqualung , as a \\"concept album\\", a label he has firmly rejected to this day. Further, he was rather miffed at having Tull labeled a progressive rock band and grouped with Yes and Emerson, Lake and Palmer.<br /><br />Anderson's response was: \\"If the critics want a concept album we'll give them the mother of all concept albums and we'll make it so bombastic and so over the top.\\" Ian Anderson has been quoted as stating that Thick as a Brick was written \\"because everyone was saying we were a progressive rock band, so we decided to live up to the reputation and write a progressive album, but done as a parody of the genre.\\" So the band created an album deliberately integrated around one concept: a poem by Gerald about the trials of growing up. Beyond this, the album was a send-up of all pretentious \\"concept albums\\". Even the title refers to someone slow to learn. But maybe the biggest joke was that it worked! the album hit number one in the U.S. The album is notable for its many musical themes, time signature changes and tempo shifts as well as the instrumentation which includes harpsichord, xylophone, timpani, violin, lute, trumpet, saxophone, and a string section. Not exactly your average blues rock lineup, ehh?<br /><br />Well here it is 40 years later and Gerald is all grown up. Anderson set out, on TAAB 2 to write songs that would update the story. \\"I wonder what the eight-year-old Gerald Bostock would be doing today. Would the fabled newspaper still exist? (it does, as a dot com of course) \\" The  album presents five very different , hypothetical life stories for Gerald Bostock, including: a greedy investment banker, a homosexual homeless man, a determined soldier, a sanctimonious church chorister, and a \\"most ordinary man\\" who (married and childless) owns a corner store; by the end of the album, however, all five possibilities seem to converge in a similar concluding moment of gloomy or pitiful solitude.<br /><br />The style of the mock-newspaper cover of the original Thick as a Brick album follows forward forty years as well, an online newspaper has been set up, simply entitled StCleve: [...] so Anderson hasn't lost his satirical sense of humor over the years. And, now that the story has come near full circle, the album asks the question how our own lives developed, and did we end up what we dreamed of being then, or are we surprised and disappointed at the result? And, are there similarities to our dreams, even though that result is different? It's an album that shines a light on those \\"what if?\\" moments us baby boomers are bound to have had.  \\"What ifs, maybes and might-of-beens, perhaps and wait and sees\\".<br /><br />Though it would be near impossible to recreate the astounding ground breaking musical strides of the 1972 original, which is one of the most successful rock albums of all time, but it would have been just as impossible to accomplish that and complete Gerald's journey. Nevertheless, Ian Anderson has managed to create a great album of \\"progressive rock\\", \\"Jethro Tull\\" music updating the story while staying true to the original. The band, even though only Anderson and Scott Hammond, the drummer, remain from the first classic session, is just as interesting and musically disciplined as the classic Tull lineup.<br /><br />The album comes in a `standard jewel case' CD, digital download , and the version I would recommend, a  Special Edition 2-Disc package with a DVD featuring 5.1 stereo mixes, 24 bit stereo, video of the making of TAAB 2, interviews with the musicians and Anderson reading the lyrics in various locations....he may even be in the village square in St. Cleve...<br /><br />The Dirty Lowdownf humor over the years. 1451627289|2.0|King creates an appealing protagonist in Jake Epping, a Maine high school English teacher, an affable flake with a good heart who loves his work, the path less traveled and underdogs of most stripes.  As the title suggests and the dust-jacket confirms, this novel is about Jake stumbling into an ability to time travel back to the 50s and 60s, and to potentially change events that have already occurred -- i.e., the JFK assassination in November 1963.  The basis for and terms of Jake's time travel are fairly clever and would form a good basis for a novella or a novel of, say, 200-250 pages.  Regrettably, this potentially good 200-250 page story is lost in an 850-page tome that moves along at a glacial pace and with a great deal of predictability.  Large sections of the book can be skipped altogether without losing any of the real substance of the story ... it's just hard to predict in advance which those sections are.  Obviously a heavy-handed editor could have saved the reader from this difficulty.  In the end, there is not enough payback in the story to justify the mind-numbing pages of plot inactivity.<br /><br />Having been a big fan of many of King's early books (Fire Starter is still my favorite), I've been very disappointed by both this book and Blockade Billy, in which King shows he can write a very poor novella as well.  I would advise sticking to his earlier books, as, for whatever reason, King currently seems to be in a real slump.

0312641893|4.0|I liked CINDER a lot - it was one of those books that kept me turning pages because of the well-paced plot and I didn't want to stop reading it. It was a little predictable, but also exciting. There were parts I thought I knew what was going to happen, and they ended up a little different, but overall I felt like I knew many of the big things were going to happen before they occurred. Now, because this is a retelling so has a basic structure of familiar things sI would look for, that's to be expected, but I wanted more of the big moments to be more a surprise to me. However, I still really enjoyed reading it, and can see that this would actually be good for my middle school students.<br /><br />This book is full of political intrigue, suspense, action, and not knowing what would happen next or how things would work out. There is an intergalactic battle brewing and it adds another whole element to the tension of the story beyond just what is going to happen to Cinder. There was strong world-building as I felt as if I was there with the characters and understood what was happening and why they were dealing with the things they were, but it wasn't overly drawn out with lengthy sections for exposition. It was worked right in and pieces were slowly revealed to the reader as the characters learned them. There are androids and cyborgs and netscreens and hovercrafts and all kinds of technological advancements which add a great science-fiction element to the story as well.<br /><br />One of my favorite parts of the book was the characters. Cinder is just a warm-hearted character - I was really pulling for her to make it through all of her difficulties. I liked her and I wanted her to come out on top in the end. She is kind and caring even though she has not received this same treatment from others in her life. She is brave, but she has no idea how brave until she is faced with some difficult choices, and realizes what she would and can do to help save people. She is finding her strength throughout this book, and she does it all with an integrity of character that is admirable. She may not be physically 100% human, but that doesn't mean that she doesn't have the feeling and thoughts of one (contrary to what some might believe).<br /><br />There were some great secondary characters (Peony, Ito, Kai) who were endearing as well and made it possible to believe that Cinder would have the will to get through some of the stuff she's going through. And, there are also the requisite evil characters who I really hated (as I was supposed to). Prince Kai is actually my favorite character and he has the start of a sweet romance going on, but he has a whole lot of other issues to deal with as well. I have to say that I would have liked even more of Kai; although, the third person writing style gives us a lot of insight into his character and feelings, but mostly from Cinder's perspective. Kai is strong and sweet and young and looking for something to help him through as he is forced to grow up really fast, and I was definitely supporting him throughout.<br /><br />The ending was intense...I'm not sure where this story could go next. It was previewed a little bit in the final conversation, but I'm still not sure what to expect from the second book. I'm glad this is going to be a quartet of books, but can't believe that means we'll have to wait until 2015 to get the end of the story! I will definitely be picking up the rest of the books though to see what happens with Cinder and Kai next!<br /><br />Review originally posted on Heise Reads & Recommendsher strength throughout this book, and she does it all with an integrity of character that is admirable. 0385344228|2.0|Landay has a good concept, but the execution fails.  The book never comes together.  The story line is relatively simple:  an Assistant District Attorney's son is accused of murder.  Although a self-made man and one of the good guys, the ADA has a family history of violence.  The premise is compelling:  does nurture or nature or neither explain a murderer?  The subtopics are is there a genetic predisposition to violence?  And do we have free will?  That's an ageless discussion which has had significant contributions from scientific work on the human brain and genetics in recent years.  Our religions and our laws presuppose that man has free will and is responsible for his decisions, and it is critical to all of our institutions that he does.  If we do not have free will, then all of our understanding about the nature of man must be seriously revised and all existing premises about ourselves scrapped.  Landay has an opinion on the subject, and the story he has selected to illustrate his opinion is definitely interesting, but he never conveys to the reader the overwhelming importance of the debate.  In the hands of a Scott Turow, it could have been a very good book and still left the reader wondering about the nurture versus nature conflict, as he should because the topic remains unsettled.<br /><br />Landay deals with the issue of nature versus nurture so superficially that the characters, despite what would cause serious soul searching and deep anguish in real people, never come to life, never show us the confusion and pain the situation would create in real people.  The characters lack depth and humanity; they are paper cut-outs, in my opinion, because Landay does not write believable dialogue.  The exchanges between characters, instead of developing character and illuminating the inner workings of the mind and heart, almost negate what he is trying to do.  I suggest that when an author decides to use first person narrative, as Landay has in this novel, he should not tackle such a difficult thesis, especially if he has a point of view contrary to millennia of discussion, debate, and experiment which he wants the reader to accept.  In first person narrative, the reader is not granted insights into the minds and hearts of other characters except through the dialogue, and if the dialogue fails, the book fails.  Although the courtroom exchanges are reasonably informative and hold attention, the private dialogue is just too stilted and wooden.  The narrator himself is insufficiently introspective for the reader to gain greater understanding of the argument between determinism and free will.  When the reader finishes the novel, I suggest that Landay has not provided sufficient depth of insight into the way the human heart works to make his conclusion or even his story line believable.  Overall the book is superficial and unconvincing; Landay was way in over his philosophical head.

0871404133|5.0|A sweeping history of life and a great read. Despite his special interest in entomology, Wilson is a great generalist who is able to combine evidence from the sciences and the humanities to give us a new look at human nature and an understanding of its consequences, both for us and the rest of life on this planet. Having evolved by both individual selection and group selection, we are torn between selfishness and altruism. These two driving forces make us what we are, for better or for worse. Defined by these opposites, we struggle for a balance between our creativity and our destructivity. \\"The brain ... is an organ not merely divided into major parts but divided against itself.\\" This dichotomy has given rise to all of our great art, music, literature and science, but it has now brought us to the brink of disaster.<br /><br />\\"The struggle to control vital resources continues globally, and it is growing worse. The problem arose because humanity failed to seize the great opportunity given it at the dawn of the Neolithic era. It might then have halted population growth below the constraining minimum limit. As a species we did the opposite, however. There was no way for us to foresee the consequences of our initial success. We simply took what was given us and continued to multiply and consume in blind obedience to instincts inherited from our humbler, more brutally constrained Paleolithic ancestors.\\"<br /><br />This book is a perfect companion to [[ASIN:193149858X Limits to Growth: The 30-Year Update]]. Using Gauguin's great painting \\"Where Did We Come From? What Are We We? Where Are We Going?\\" as an outline, Wilson, in tackling that last question, comes to the same conclusions as the authors of Limits, who first warned us of them back in 1972, that we are \\"destroying our birthplace\\":<br /><br />\\"The evidence for climate warming, with industrial pollution as the principal cause, is now overwhelming. Also evident upon even casual inspection is the rapid disappearance of tropical forests and grasslands and other habitats where most of the diversity of life exists. If global changes caused by HIPPO (Habitat destruction, Invasive species, Pollution, Overpopulation, and Overharvesting, in that order of importance) are not abated, half the species of plants and animals could be extinct or at least among the 'living dead'--about to become extinct--by the end of the century. We are needlessly turning the gold we inherited from our forebears into straw, and for that we will be despised by our descendants.\\"<br /><br />After acknowledging that myths and gods have been the well springs of much great art, Wilson rightly condemns today's organized religions because \\"they encourage ignorance, distract people from recognizing problems of the real world, and often lead them in wrong directions into disastrous actions.\\" But then he confesses to his own blind faith: \\"Earth, by the twenty-second century, can be turned, if we so wish, into a permanent paradise for human beings, or at least the strong beginnings of one. We will do a lot more damage to ourselves and the rest of life along the way, but out of an ethic of simple decency to one another, the unrelenting application of reason, and acceptance of what we truly are, our dreams will finally come home to stay.\\"<br /><br />Unfortunately this faith is not supported by the evidence. Wilson is not taking into consideration the rapidly closing window of opportunity to turn things around. As Limits to Growth shows, population, non-renewable resource depletion and pollution are growing exponentially, overshooting the ability of the global ecosystem to sustain them. Their computer models, using current data, show a collapse of the industrial system around 2050, just as their 1972 models did. The one thing Wilson overlooked in his examination of Homo sapiens' evolution is that our brains and our cultures have developed during long periods when rates of change were very slow compared to the present. We're not wired to deal with problems beyond our own generation. By the time our politicians and corporations see it coming, it will be too late. Yes, we have the technology to begin converting to long-term sustainability right now, but it's not happening. We are creatures of the moment. Selfishness comes first, altruism later, both for individuals and nations. If it were otherwise, as Wilson says, we would be social robots like the ants. But \\"acceptance of what we truly are\\" may be our only hope, and in that sense books like these are of great value.re not wired to deal with problems beyond our own generation. By the time our politicians and corporations see it coming, it will be too late. Yes, we have the technology to begin converting to long-term sustainability right now, but it's not happening. We are creatures of the moment. Selfishness comes first, altruism later, both for individuals and nations. If it were otherwise, as Wilson says, we would be social robots like the ants. But \\"acceptance of what we truly are\\" may be our only hope, and in that sense books like these are of great value.

1451683405|4.0|The author immediately takes the side that there is not \\"free will\\", he states his case in a very adamant way perhaps intentionally trying to shock those that believe in an independent free `soul'.  The author has a very limited definition of what it means to have free will, requiring the ability to create action without any influence from prior events or background conditions, i.e. to create something out of nothing.<br /><br />Obviously this definition of free will would cause anyone who thinks about it much to agree that this does not exist.  We are not isolated little gods that are immune from cause and effect.  And yet the author himself admits a \\"mysterious\\" process where thoughts \\"appear\\" with the cause at least unknowable, if not without cause.  I would suggest that whole idea of free will (as defined by the author) only applies if you view humans as independent beings (i.e. having a unchanging `soul' unconnected to nature or events).  When humans are view as a process, as part of a continuum, the idea of the \\"self\\" changes radically and free will becomes a creativity that does in fact just \\"appear\\" seemingly from nothing.  The idea of cause and effect being only relevant in timeline (which our human view is captured in).  When time is not a factor, things can `appear' without cause; as in the cause with quantum mechanics or the Big Bang \\"beginning\\" of the universe.<br /><br />Regardless of your current views, the book is well worth the read.  Perhaps even especially if you disagree with the author - challenging your own ideas is always worth the aggravation.
